Regulation of CAM is complex, and CAM-related enzymes use different regulatory mechanisms, which explains how CAM evolved independently many times during evolution: in non-CAM plants, the gene content encoding the enzymatic machinery is present, but diel expression patterns are likely silenced or not activated sufficiently at the <i>cis</i>-acting, cell-specific individual gene level. This work provides the first detailed analysis of the expression and regulation patterns of genes associated with CAM and could ultimately be used to engineer better WUE and drought tolerance in crop plants.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec12" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec12-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec12">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec12-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">Nuclear DNA preparation.</h3><p>Fresh leaf tissues from pineapple varieties F153 and MD2 and wild species <i>A. bracteatus</i> accession CB5 were collected, and nuclear DNA was isolated following the procedure described previously<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Ming, R. et al. Genome of the long-living sacred lotus (Nelumbo nucifera Gaertn.). Genome Biol. 14, R41 (2013)." href="/articles/ng.3435#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d43724e2590">43</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec14">Flow cytometry analysis of CB5 genome size.</h3><p>The procedure used to analyze the nuclear DNA content of <i>A. bracteatus</i> accession CB5 was described previously<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="VanBuren, R. et al. Longli is not a hybrid of Longan and Lychee as revealed by genome size analysis and trichome morphology. Trop. Plant Biol. 4, 228–236 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.3435#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d43724e2605">44</a></sup>. The nuclear DNA content estimated by flow cytometry reflects the diploid, or 2<i>C</i>, genome size, but for sequencing purposes the haploid, or 1<i>C</i>, value is used and converted to the number of nucleotides using the equation 1 pg = 978 Mb (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Dolezel, J., Bartos, J., Voglmayr, H. &amp; Greilhuber, J. Nuclear DNA content and genome size of trout and human. Cytometry A 51, 127–128, author reply 129 (2003)." href="/articles/ng.3435#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d43724e2614">45</a>). The estimated genome size of <i>A. bracteatus</i> CB5 is 1<i>C</i> = 592 Mb (2<i>C</i> = 1.21 pg), close to the previously reported flow cytometry–based value of 1<i>C</i> = 526 Mb (for <i>A. comosus</i>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Arumuganathan, K. &amp; Earle, E. Nuclear DNA content of some important plant species. Plant Mol. Biol. Rep. 9, 208–218 (1991)." href="/articles/ng.3435#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d43724e2634">8</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec15">Genome sequencing.</h3><p>Raw sequences for F153 were generated primarily using Illumina sequencing, following a standard protocol, with the HiSeq 2000 platform. Four paired-end libraries were created with inserts of 180 bp, 500 bp, 3 kb and 8 kb, generating 33×, 150×, 41.8× and 25.5× coverage, respectively. A paired-end 20-kb insert library was generated for scaffolding using the Roche/454 circularization protocol with sequencing carried out on the 454 FLX+ platform. We generated 1.2 Gb of sequence (<span class="stix">∼</span>2× coverage) using Moleculo chemistry, with average read lengths of 5 kb, and 2.8 Gb (<span class="stix">∼</span>5× coverage) using PacBio P6-C4 chemistry. A total of 9,400 BACs were sequenced using a random pooling strategy with 48 BACs per pool. Each pool was sequenced to produce Illumina HiSeq 2500 paired-end 2 × 150 bp sequencing.</p><p>Raw sequences for MD2 and CB5 were generated using Illumina sequencing, following standard protocols, with the HiSeq 2000 platform (for 180-bp and 500-bp insert libraries) and the HiSeq 2500 platform (for 3-kb and 8-kb insert libraries). The four paired-end libraries with inserts of 180 bp, 500 bp, 3 kb and 8 kb were sequenced to produce 33×, 50×, 35× and 12× coverage, respectively.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec16">Genome assembly.</h3><p><i>Genome assembly overview.</i> The pineapple reference genome assembly incorporated data from a mixture of sequencing technologies, including whole-genome shotgun sequencing with Illumina, 454, PacBio and Moleculo technologies, as well as BAC pools sequenced with Illumina sequencing. The assembly underwent three major rounds of improvement by applying the different technologies (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3435#Fig14">Supplementary Fig. 10</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3435#MOESM29">Supplementary Table 17</a>). The original F153 pineapple assembly was based on ALLPATHS-LG using Illumina whole-genome sequencing and 454 sequencing data (v1 assembly), was subsequently improved by incorporating the assembled BAC contigs (v2 assembly) and was finally improved by incorporating the PacBio and Moleculo data (v3 assembly). See the <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3435#MOESM29">Supplementary Note</a> for a detailed description of the assembly methods. The <i>k</i>-mer coverage of the F153 fragment library is shown in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3435#Fig15">Supplementary Figure 11</a>.</p><p><i>Genetic maps and chromosomal assembly.</i> Ninety-three F<sub>1</sub> individuals from a CB5 × F153 cross were sequenced to an average depth of 10× by whole-genome sequencing. The raw reads were mapped onto the genome assembly using Bowtie2. Segregating polymorphic SNPs were called using the Genome Analysis Toolkit (GATK). Only SNPs that were homozygous for the reference genotype in one parent and heterozygous in the other parent were used. SNPs were assigned to either an F153 or CB5 map depending on which parental genotype was heterozygous. The SNPs segregating for each parent were further divided into two pools using genotyping calls for a single F<sub>1</sub> individual that was sequenced to higher coverage (50×), to group the SNPs according to the phase of the SNP. Using the deep sequenced F<sub>1</sub> individual allowed all of the SNPs that contained the non-reference SNP on the same chromosome to be grouped together.</p><p>Individual SNPs had a high rate of missing data, and many heterozygous SNPs also could be scored incorrectly because of limited depth of sequencing coverage; however, by looking at several adjacent SNPs, a consensus SNP genotype for each scaffold piece could be determined. Adjacent SNPs mapping to 100-kb bins on each scaffold were combined into a consensus genotype for each individual. Scaffold segments 100 kb long with ≥15 SNPs had consensus genotypes determined. These consensus genotypes were ordered into genetic maps. The F153 map consisted of 3,125 scaffold segments containing 928,659 segregating SNPs and was assembled into the 25 linkage groups corresponding to the haploid chromosome number of pineapple. A consensus order for the scaffolds in chromosomal pseudomolecules was determined, with breakpoints (represented as intervals) approximated using the information from individual SNPs. Chimeric scaffolds were split at the largest gap in the inferred breakpoints. When no gaps could be found within the inferred breakpoints, the closest gap was identified instead.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec17">Genome annotation.</h3><p>We used MAKER to generate a first-pass gene annotation. MAKER is a computational pipeline for genome annotation that can integrate multiple tiers of coding evidence, including <i>ab initio</i> gene predictions, transcript evidence and protein evidence<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Cantarel, B.L. et al. MAKER: an easy-to-use annotation pipeline designed for emerging model organism genomes. Genome Res. 18, 188–196 (2008)." href="/articles/ng.3435#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d43724e2700">9</a></sup>. <i>Ab initio</i> gene models were evaluated against matching transcript and protein evidence to select the model for each gene that was most consistent on the basis of an AED metric<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Cantarel, B.L. et al. MAKER: an easy-to-use annotation pipeline designed for emerging model organism genomes. Genome Res. 18, 188–196 (2008)." href="/articles/ng.3435#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d43724e2707">9</a></sup>.</p><p>Input data for MAKER were prepared as follows. First, <i>ab initio</i> gene predictors, including SNAP<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Korf, I. Gene finding in novel genomes. BMC Bioinformatics 5, 59 (2004)." href="/articles/ng.3435#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d43724e2717">46</a></sup>, GENEMARK<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Lomsadze, A., Ter-Hovhannisyan, V., Chernoff, Y.O. &amp; Borodovsky, M. Gene identification in novel eukaryotic genomes by self-training algorithm. Nucleic Acids Res. 33, 6494–6506 (2005)." href="/articles/ng.3435#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d43724e2721">47</a></sup> and AUGUSTUS<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Stanke, M., Schoffmann, O., Morgenstern, B. &amp; Waack, S. Gene prediction in eukaryotes with a generalized hidden Markov model that uses hints from external sources. BMC Bioinformatics 7, 62 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.3435#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d43724e2725">48</a></sup>, were each trained with nearly 'full-length' pineapple transcripts. The pineapple transcripts were constructed using PASA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Haas, B.J. et al. Automated eukaryotic gene structure annotation using EVidenceModeler and the Program to Assemble Spliced Alignments. Genome Biol. 9, R7 (2008)." href="/articles/ng.3435#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d43724e2729">49</a></sup> and were evaluated against UniProt plant proteins to identify the set of nearly full-length candidates that covered at least 95% of any target protein. The pineapple transcripts were sampled from major tissues, including flower, fruit, leaf and root. Comprehensive transcriptome assembly was carried out using both <i>de novo</i> Trinity and reference-guided Trinity<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Grabherr, M.G. et al. Full-length transcriptome assembly from RNA-Seq data without a reference genome. Nat. Biotechnol. 29, 644–652 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.3435#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d43724e2737">50</a></sup>, with the results combined and used together as mRNA evidence for MAKER. Plant proteins were downloaded from UniProt (last accessed on 21 September 2014) and used as plant-specific evidence for MAKER.</p><p>MAKER was run on the pineapple v3 scaffold assembly with the above evidence twice, once with and once without masking with a pineapple-specific repeat library, for the purpose of comparison. Putative proteins over 30 amino acids in length were kept. Additionally, we set up a JBrowse instance<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Skinner, M.E., Uzilov, A.V., Stein, L.D., Mungall, C.J. &amp; Holmes, I.H. JBrowse: a next-generation genome browser. Genome Res. 19, 1630–1638 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.3435#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d43724e2744">51</a></sup> during structural annotation to visualize the gene models along with the aligned annotation evidence (see <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="section anchor" href="/articles/ng.3435#Sec27">URLs</a>).</p><p>For the final gene set, a MAKER run without repeat masking was selected, followed by extensive filtering of TE-related genes. The original MAKER run produced 31,893 genes, from which we removed 4,850 TE-related genes and 19 that were broken during linkage group construction. For the 27,024 remaining genes, we obtained 24,063 (89.0%) complete gene models, with 11% categorized as partial (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3435#MOESM30">Supplementary Table 5</a>).</p><p>For functional annotation, we inferred the human readable protein description for each predicted pineapple protein using AHRD (see <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="section anchor" href="/articles/ng.3435#Sec27">URLs</a>), on the basis of names from three protein databases: SWISS-PROT, TrEMBL and TAIR10. The InterPro domains, Gene Ontology (GO) terms and KEGG pathway information associated with each protein were computed using InterProScan<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Zdobnov, E.M. &amp; Apweiler, R. InterProScan—an integration platform for the signature-recognition methods in InterPro. Bioinformatics 17, 847–848 (2001)." href="/articles/ng.3435#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d43724e2764">52</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec18">Synteny analysis.</h3><p>We performed synteny searches to compare the pineapple genome structure with that of other related plant genomes. To call syntenic blocks, we performed all-against-all LAST<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Kielbasa, S.M., Wan, R., Sato, K., Horton, P. &amp; Frith, M.C. Adaptive seeds tame genomic sequence comparison. Genome Res. 21, 487–493 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.3435#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d43724e2776">53</a></sup> and chained the LAST hits with a distance cutoff of 20 genes, also requiring at least four gene pairs per syntenic block using QUOTA-ALIGN<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Tang, H. et al. Screening synteny blocks in pairwise genome comparisons through integer programming. BMC Bioinformatics 12, 102 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.3435#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d43724e2780">54</a></sup>. Synteny was searched for by performing comparisons of the pineapple genome with other selected genomes (<i>Amborella</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Amborella Genome Project. The Amborella genome and the evolution of flowering plants. Science 342, 1241089 (2013)." href="/articles/ng.3435#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d43724e2786">19</a></sup>, banana<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="D'Hont, A. et al. The banana (Musa acuminata) genome and the evolution of monocotyledonous plants. Nature 488, 213–217 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.3435#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d43724e2790">18</a></sup>, date palm<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Al-Dous, E.K. et al. De novo genome sequencing and comparative genomics of date palm (Phoenix dactylifera). Nat. Biotechnol. 29, 521–527 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.3435#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d43724e2794">55</a></sup>, duckweed<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Wang, W. et al. The Spirodela polyrhiza genome reveals insights into its neotenous reduction fast growth and aquatic lifestyle. Nat. Commun. 5, 3311 (2014)." href="/articles/ng.3435#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d43724e2799">17</a></sup>, grape<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Jaillon, O. et al. The grapevine genome sequence suggests ancestral hexaploidization in major angiosperm phyla. Nature 449, 463–467 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.3435#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d43724e2803">15</a></sup>, oil palm<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Singh, R. et al. Oil palm genome sequence reveals divergence of interfertile species in Old and New worlds. Nature 500, 335–339 (2013)." href="/articles/ng.3435#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d43724e2807">56</a></sup>, orchid<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Cai, J. et al. The genome sequence of the orchid Phalaenopsis equestris. Nat. Genet. 47, 65–72 (2015)." href="/articles/ng.3435#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d43724e2811">20</a></sup>, rice<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="International Rice Genome Sequencing Project. The map-based sequence of the rice genome. Nature 436, 793–800 (2005)." href="/articles/ng.3435#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d43724e2815">57</a></sup> and sorghum<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Paterson, A.H. et al. The Sorghum bicolor genome and the diversification of grasses. Nature 457, 551–556 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.3435#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d43724e2819">58</a></sup>). The resulting dot plots were inspected to confirm the paleoploidy level of pineapple in relation to the other genomes by counting the syntenic depth at each genomic region.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec19">Phylogenetic dating of whole-genome duplication events.</h3><p>We used an integrated pipeline of spatial and temporal evidence to circumscribe WGD events<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Jiao, Y., Li, J., Tang, H. &amp; Paterson, A.H. Integrated syntenic and phylogenomic analyses reveal an ancient genome duplication in monocots. Plant Cell Online 26, 2792–2802 (2014)." href="/articles/ng.3435#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d43724e2831">16</a></sup>. Briefly, we started with homologous gene detection and then used two parallel methodologies to provide separate lines of evidence to place the events on the tree of life. The 'spatial' signal relies on extracted syntenic blocks (macrosynteny) and gene order alignments (microsynteny). Analysis of synteny patterns was conducted using CoGe comparative genomics tools (see <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="section anchor" href="/articles/ng.3435#Sec27">URLs</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Lyons, E. et al. Finding and comparing syntenic regions among Arabidopsis and the outgroups papaya, poplar, and grape: CoGe with rosids. Plant Physiol. 148, 1772–1781 (2008)." href="/articles/ng.3435#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d43724e2838">59</a></sup>. The inferred syntenic depth ratio of syntenic blocks allows determination of the paleopolyploidy level<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Tang, H., Bowers, J.E., Wang, X. &amp; Paterson, A.H. Angiosperm genome comparisons reveal early polyploidy in the monocot lineage. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 107, 472–477 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.3435#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d43724e2842">13</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Wang, W. et al. The Spirodela polyrhiza genome reveals insights into its neotenous reduction fast growth and aquatic lifestyle. Nat. Commun. 5, 3311 (2014)." href="/articles/ng.3435#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d43724e2845">17</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Tang, H. et al. Screening synteny blocks in pairwise genome comparisons through integer programming. BMC Bioinformatics 12, 102 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.3435#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d43724e2848">54</a></sup>. The 'temporal' signal relies on the construction of gene families using sequence similarity. A clustering of coalescence among gene duplicates was used to infer likely genome-wide events<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Jiao, Y., Li, J., Tang, H. &amp; Paterson, A.H. Integrated syntenic and phylogenomic analyses reveal an ancient genome duplication in monocots. Plant Cell Online 26, 2792–2802 (2014)." href="/articles/ng.3435#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d43724e2852">16</a></sup>. The latter method is supplemented by using only the syntenic gene pairs in the structural data set to offer higher precision during inference.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec20">Ancestral chromosome reconstruction.</h3><p>We identified syntenic blocks for duckweed and pineapple using the SynMap procedure (default settings) on the CoGe platform<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Lyons, E. et al. Finding and comparing syntenic regions among Arabidopsis and the outgroups papaya, poplar, and grape: CoGe with rosids. Plant Physiol. 148, 1772–1781 (2008)." href="/articles/ng.3435#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d43724e2864">59</a></sup>. For the many duckweed regions showing fourfold matching blocks in pineapple, we identified the start and end points of the four matching regions in pineapple. We then examined all duplicates found in pineapple versus pineapple SynMap analysis, without restricting ourselves to duplicates satisfying the strict syntenic block conditions. From these data, we extracted all sets of gene quadruples consisting of two pairs of close matches (generally &gt;73% identical) with the four interpair matches scoring lower (&lt;74% identical). Almost all of these gene quadruples fell into the pattern consisting of seven sets of four chromosomes (or large chromosomal segments).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec21">Gene family analysis.</h3><p>Gene models were sorted into gene families circumscribed from 22 plant genomes using BLASTX (best BLAST hit; <i>e</i>-value cutoff of 1 × 10<sup>−10</sup>) (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Amborella Genome Project. The Amborella genome and the evolution of flowering plants. Science 342, 1241089 (2013)." href="/articles/ng.3435#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d43724e2880">19</a>). The taxa used to estimate gene family circumscriptions (orthogroups) are represented in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3435#Fig3">Figure 3</a>. Putative paralog pairs from both the σ (2,750 pairs) and τ (1,292 pairs) WGD events were used to collapse gene families that were improperly split. The peptide sequences for each corrected gene family were aligned using default settings for peptides in PASTA (v1.6.4)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Mirarab, S., Nguyen, N. &amp; Warnow, T. in Research in Computational Molecular Biology 177–191 (Springer, 2015)." href="/articles/ng.3435#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d43724e2887">60</a></sup>. Peptide alignments were then converted to the corresponding codon alignments using pal2nal<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Suyama, M., Torrents, D. &amp; Bork, P. PAL2NAL: robust conversion of protein sequence alignments into the corresponding codon alignments. Nucleic Acids Res. 34, W609–W612 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.3435#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d43724e2892">61</a></sup>. Gene trees for the nucleotide alignments were estimated using RAxML v7.3.0 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Stamatakis, A. RAxML-VI-HPC: maximum likelihood–based phylogenetic analyses with thousands of taxa and mixed models. Bioinformatics 22, 2688–2690 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.3435#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d43724e2895">62</a>), rooting to <i>Amborella</i>, <i>Vitis vinifera</i> or <i>A. thaliana</i> with a generalized time reversible (GTR) + Γ model over 500 bootstraps.</p><p>The relationships among species, as denoted in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3435#Fig3">Figure 3</a>, were used to generate hypotheses to test for the presence of a polyploid event along the phylogeny. For each node along the backbone of the monocot clade, a hypothesis was generated that consisted of taxa descendent of the node, which would share the polyploid event, and taxa in the sister group to the node, which would not share the polyploid event. Gene trees were queried using putative paralogs from both the σ and τ duplications. For each pair of genes, the last common ancestor (LCA) node was identified, and the taxa descendent of the node and those found in the sister group were compared to the expectation with the generated hypotheses. If a hypothesis matched what was found for the node, then the bootstrap value of that node was used to count support for the event. For each node, we report LCA nodes that had bootstrap values of 80 or greater and those that had bootstrap values of less than 80 but greater than 50 (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3435#Fig12">Supplementary Fig. 8</a>).</p><p>We identified 697 LCA nodes for putative σ paralogs across 986 gene trees that had bootstrap values of 50 or greater. Of these nodes, 359 were found to represent well-defined hypotheses with sampling of taxa from both the descendent and sister groups. We note that the reduction in LCA nodes is due to the conservative nature of the hypothesis-testing algorithm, which requires sampling of representative taxa for both descendent and sister groups. Of these LCA nodes, 258 (159 with bootstrap values ≥80 and 99 with bootstrap values ≥50) placed the σ WGD before the divergence of pineapple from the rest of the Poales, which represents the overwhelming majority (71.9%) of the LCA nodes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3435#Fig12">Supplementary Fig. 8</a>). The next best-supported timing for the σ WGD was on the lineage leading to the commelinids, with estimated duplications in just 42 gene trees (11.7%).</p><p>A total of 192 LCA nodes for putative τ paralogs were found in 361 gene trees that had bootstrap values of 50 or greater. We identified 83 LCA nodes that represented well-defined hypotheses. The predominantly supported placement of the τ WGD was shown to be after the divergence of the <i>Spirodela</i> (Alismatales) lineage from the rest of the monocots and was shared by Asparagales, commelinids and Poales with a total of 45 LCA nodes (28 with bootstrap values ≥80 and 17 with bootstrap values ≥50), or 54.2% of the LCA nodes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3435#Fig14">Supplementary Fig. 10</a>). The second best-supported timing for the τ WGD was on the lineage leading to the common ancestor of monocots and eudocots, with estimated duplications in just 23 gene trees (27.7%).</p><p>In summary, phylogenomic analyses support the results of the comparative synteny analyses, placing the σ WGD on the lineage leading to the Poales crown group and the τ WGD between the divergences of Alismatales and Asparagales from the lineage leading to the commelinids.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec22">Plant materials for CAM photosynthesis analysis.</h3><p>Leaves from <i>A. comosus</i> cultivar MD2 were collected from the field at Dole Plantation (Wahiawa, Hawaii) for RNA extraction and physiology studies. The D leaf (the youngest physiologically mature leaf, fourth from the apex) was collected from five individual plants as one replicate, and three biological replicates were collected every hour between 10 a.m. on 24 October 2013 and 9 a.m. on 25 October 2013, with the exception of two time points at 2 p.m. on 24 October and 1 a.m. on 25 October. The sunset time on 24 October was 6 p.m. HST, and the sunrise time on 25 October was 6:32 a.m. HST. For the time-course experiment, two regions of the D leaf were used for transcriptomic studies: the white base and green tip. Thirteen time points (6 p.m., 8 p.m., 10 p.m., midnight, 2 a.m., 4 a.m., 6 a.m., 8 a.m., 10 a.m., noon, 1 p.m., 3 p.m. and 4 p.m.) were chosen for RNA-seq library construction. All leaf segments were frozen in liquid nitrogen immediately after they were collected in the field and were stored at −80 °C until ground into powder in liquid nitrogen.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec23">RNA extraction and library construction.</h3><p>Total RNA was extracted from ground leaf using the Qiagen RNeasy Plant Mini kit (74904), following the manufacturer's protocol. DNA was then removed using the DNA-free DNA Removal kit (Life Technologies, AM1906M). A single indexed RNA-seq library was constructed using the Illumina TruSeq Stranded RNA Sample Preparation kit (RS-122-2001) and then sequenced using the Illumina HiSeq 2500 platform in paired-end 100-nt mode. Three biological replicates were studied for each time point.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec24">Sequencing read processing and estimation of gene expression.</h3><p>The trimmed paired-end reads for each sample were aligned to repeat-masked pineapple assembly v3 using TopHat v2.0.9 with default settings<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Trapnell, C. et al. Differential gene and transcript expression analysis of RNA-seq experiments with TopHat and Cufflinks. Nat. Protoc. 7, 562–578 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.3435#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d43724e2964">63</a></sup>. The normalized FPKM value for each sample was estimated by Cufflinks v2.2.1, followed by Cuffnorm v2.2.1, using the default setting with pineapple gene model annotation provided (-g option).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec25">Gene network construction using diurnal expression profiles.</h3><p>Gene regulatory networks for white leaf base and green leaf tip tissues, designated as the 'base network' and 'tip network', respectively, were constructed using the PCA-CMI algorithm on the basis of gene expression data, and 15,483 genes (201,537 interactions) and 13,543 genes (188,391 interactions) were included in the base and tip networks, respectively. Isolated interactions were trimmed, and only the largest module was retained in which 11,079 genes (183,168 interactions, or 90.9% of the 201,537 interactions) and 7,506 genes (177,308 interactions, or 94.1% of the 188,391 interactions) were present in the base and tip networks, respectively. Topology analysis was then conducted for the two networks using the MCL algorithm.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec26"><i>Cis</i> element annotation and enrichment analysis.</h3><p>Five known circadian clock–related motif sequences were searched for in the 1-kb promoter sequences upstream of pineapple genes involved in carbon fixation. These five motifs included the morning element (CCACAC), the evening element (AAAATATCT), the CCA1-binding site (AAAAATCT), the G-box element (G-box; CACGTG) and the TCP15-binding motif (TCP15; NGGNCCCAC)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Michael, T.P. et al. Network discovery pipeline elucidates conserved time-of-day–specific cis-regulatory modules. PLoS Genet. 4, e14 (2008)." href="/articles/ng.3435#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d43724e2987">39</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Hudson, M.E. &amp; Quail, P.H. Identification of promoter motifs involved in the network of phytochrome A–regulated gene expression by combined analysis of genomic sequence and microarray data. Plant Physiol. 133, 1605–1616 (2003)." href="/articles/ng.3435#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d43724e2990">64</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Franco-Zorrilla, J.M. et al. DNA-binding specificities of plant transcription factors and their potential to define target genes. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 111, 2367–2372 (2014)." href="/articles/ng.3435#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d43724e2993">65</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Michael, T.P. &amp; McClung, C.R. Phase-specific circadian clock regulatory elements in Arabidopsis. Plant Physiol. 130, 627–638 (2002)." href="/articles/ng.3435#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d43724e2996">66</a></sup>. Enrichment of <i>cis</i>-regulatory elements in the promoters of photosynthetic genes in comparison to non-photosynthetic genes was tested using Fisher's exact test. The βCA genes from the orchid, rice, maize and sorghum genomes were annotated on the basis of sequence homology using BLASTP, and their promoter regions were searched for <i>cis</i>-regulatory element motif sequences. The sequences of all genomes used in the comparisons were downloaded from Phytozome v10.1, except for that of orchid, which was downloaded separately (see <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="section anchor" href="/articles/ng.3435#Sec27">URLs</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec27">URLs.</h3><p>JBrowse instance to visualize the gene models and aligned annotation evidence, <a href="http://peach.fafu.edu.cn/html/jbrowse/JBrowse-1.11.5/?data=Pineapple_genome_project">http://peach.fafu.edu.cn/html/jbrowse/JBrowse-1.11.5/?data=Pineapple_genome_project</a>; Phytozome v10.1, <a href="http://phytozome.jgi.doe.gov/">http://phytozome.jgi.doe.gov/</a>; orchid genome download, <a href="ftp://ftp.genomics.org.cn/from_BGISZ/20130120/">ftp://ftp.genomics.org.cn/from_BGISZ/20130120/</a>; Automated Assignment of Human Readable Descriptions (AHRD), <a href="https://github.com/groupschoof/AHRD/">https://github.com/groupschoof/AHRD/</a>; CoGe, <a href="http://genomevolution.org/">http://genomevolution.org/</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec28">Accession codes.</h3><p>The pineapple genome sequence, annotation and RNA-seq data have been deposited at the iPlant CoGe database and can be downloaded from <a href="https://genomevolution.org/CoGe/NotebookView.pl?nid=937">https://genomevolution.org/CoGe/NotebookView.pl?nid=937</a>. Pineapple tissue RNA-seq data and pineapple time-course RNA-seq data are available from <a href="https://de.iplantcollaborative.org/de/?type=data&amp;folder=/iplant/home/cmwai/coge_data/Pineapple_tissue_RNAseq">https://de.iplantcollaborative.org/de/?type=data&amp;folder=/iplant/home/cmwai/coge_data/Pineapple_tissue_RNAseq</a>. Pineapple genome resources are also available in Phytozome.</p></div></div></section>

mapped to the top 40 most highly expressed LTR retrotransposon families. Family names are shown as row labels, and tissue names are given as column labels. From top to bottom, the rows are sorted by total counts of mapped reads in families.</p></div></div><div class="c-article-supplementary__item js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="supp-item" id="Fig7"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="/articles/ng.3435/figures/7" data-supp-info-image="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.3435/MediaObjects/41588_2015_Article_BFng3435_Fig7_ESM.jpg">Supplementary Figure 3 Expression of subfamilies of LTR retrotransposons in nine pineapple tissue samples.</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p>The heat map shows the number of RNA-seq reads mapped to the top ten most highly expressed LTR retrotransposon families. Each row represents a subfamily, and each column represents a tissue. The numbers following family names give subfamily IDs. From top to bottom, the rows are sorted by total counts of mapped reads in families. Within each family, the rows are further sorted by total counts of mapped reads in subfamilies.</p></div></div><div class="c-article-supplementary__item js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="supp-item" id="Fig8"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="/articles/ng.3435/figures/8" data-supp-info-image="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.3435/MediaObjects/41588_2015_Article_BFng3435_Fig8_ESM.jpg">Supplementary Figure 4 Synonymous substitutions per site (<i>K</i>s) values between inferred whole-genome duplicates in pineapple.</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p>(<b>a</b>) Syntenic dot plot in pineapple versus pineapple comparison, with <i>K</i>s values color coded; only the gene pairs with a <i>K</i>s value between 0 and 2 are plotted. For clarity, only gene pairs within synteny blocks of at least size 4 are shown.</p></div></div><div class="c-article-supplementary__item js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="supp-item" id="Fig10"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="/articles/ng.3435/figures/10" data-supp-info-image="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.3435/MediaObjects/41588_2015_Article_BFng3435_Fig10_ESM.jpg">Supplementary Figure 6 Microsynteny fractionation for 4:1 pineapple to <i>Amborella</i>, providing evidence that pineapple has undergone two WGDs in its lineage since their divergence.</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p>Five exemplar regions are shown. Each panel contains multiple parallel tracks representing syntenic regions in rice and pineapple. Connecting lines show sequence similarities between the regions. CoGe, <a href="https://genomevolution.org/r/e426">https://genomevolution.org/r/e426</a>, <a href="https://genomevolution.org/r/e428">https://genomevolution.org/r/e428</a>, <a href="https://genomevolution.org/r/e427">https://genomevolution.org/r/e427</a>, <a href="https://genomevolution.org/r/e448">https://genomevolution.org/r/e448</a> and <a href="https://genomevolution.org/r/e446">https://genomevolution.org/r/e446</a>.</p></div></div><div class="c-article-supplementary__item js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="supp-item" id="Fig11"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="/articles/ng.3435/figures/11" data-supp-info-image="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.3435/MediaObjects/41588_2015_Article_BFng3435_Fig11_ESM.jpg">Supplementary Figure 7 Microsynteny fractionation for 1:2 pineapple to rice, providing evidence that rice has undergone one WGD in its lineage (ρ) since its divergence from pineapple.</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p>Three exemplar regions are shown. Each panel contains multiple parallel tracks representing syntenic regions in <i>Amborella</i> and pineapple. Connecting lines show sequence similarities between the regions. CoGe, <a href="https://genomevolution.org/r/e3kg">https://genomevolution.org/r/e3kg</a>, <a href="https://genomevolution.org/r/e3kw">https://genomevolution.org/r/e3kw</a>, <a href="https://genomevolution.org/r/e3k4">https://genomevolution.org/r/e3k4</a>.</p></div></div><div class="c-article-supplementary__item js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="supp-item" id="Fig12"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="/articles/ng.3435/figures/12" data-supp-info-image="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.3435/MediaObjects/41588_2015_Article_BFng3435_Fig12_ESM.jpg">Supplementary Figure 8 Dating of whole-genome duplication (WGD) events on the flowering plant tree.</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p>Letters represent previously identified WGDs. Estimated gene family phylogenies including genes on syntenic blocks corresponding to the σ and τ WGDs were queried to identify the timing of implied gene duplications relative to speciation events. The numbers below each lineage in the monocot clade represent gene duplication events corresponding to the σ (green) and t (purple) synteny blocks. Trees with inferred duplication events supported by greater than 80% (left) and between 80% and 50% (right) bootstrap support values are shown for each node. Taxon names are color coded as in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3435#Fig2">Figure 2</a>.</p></div></div><div class="c-article-supplementary__item js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="supp-item" id="Fig13"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="/articles/ng.3435/figures/13" data-supp-info-image="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.3435/MediaObjects/41588_2015_Article_BFng3435_Fig13_ESM.jpg">Supplementary Figure 9 Property of leaf green tip gene interaction network.</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p>(<b>a</b>,<b>c</b>,<b>d</b>) Distributions of the node degree, diameter and betweenness attribute. (<b>b</b>) Relationship between node degree and frequency in logarithmic coordinates.</p></div></div><div class="c-article-supplementary__item js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="supp-item" id="Fig14"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="/articles/ng.3435/figures/14" data-supp-info-image="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.3435/MediaObjects/41588_2015_Article_BFng3435_Fig14_ESM.jpg">Supplementary Figure 10 Schematic workflow of the pineapple genome assembly and improvement.</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p>
                        </p></div></div><div class="c-article-supplementary__item js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="supp-item" id="Fig15"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="/articles/ng.3435/figures/15" data-supp-info-image="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.3435/MediaObjects/41588_2015_Article_BFng3435_Fig15_ESM.jpg">Supplementary Figure 11 <i>k</i>-mer coverage of the F153 fragment library (<i>k</i> = 23).</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p>
